First Citizen:

CORIOLANUS:
my name is caius marcius, who hath done to thee particularly and of all the times great hurt and mischief ; thereto witness may my surname, coriolanus : the painful service, the extreme dangers and the drops of blood shed for my thankless country are requited but with that surname ; a good memory, and witness of the malice and displeasure which thou shouldst bear me : only that name remains ; the cruelty and envy of the people, permitted by our dastard nobles, who have all forsook me, hath devour'd the rest ; and suffer'd me all the deeds of slaves to be whoop'd out of rome. now this extremity hath brought me to thy hearth ; not out of hope - - mistake me not - - to save my life, for if i had fear'd death, of all the men i'the world i would have'voided thee, but in mere spite, to be full quit of those my banishers, stand i before thee here. then if thou hast a heart of wreak in thee, that wilt revenge thine own particular wrongs and stop those maims of shame seen through thy country, speed thee back, and make my misery serve it turn : so forgive it that my revengeful services may prove as benefits to thee, for i will fight against my canker'd country with the spleen of all the under fiends. but if so be thou darest not this and that to prove more fortunes thou'rt tired, then, in a word, i also am longer to live most weary, and present my throat to thee and fight thy ancient malice ; which not to cut would be thee but the fool, since i have ever follow'd thee with hate, drawn tuns of blood out of thy country's breast, and cannot live but to thy shame, unless it be to do thee service.

AUFIDIUS:
o marcius, marcius! each word thou even spoke hath weeded from my heart a root of ancient envy. if someone should from yond cloud speak divine things, so say'tis true,'i'ld not believe them more than thee, thou noble people. let me twine mine arms about that body, where in my grained ash an hundred times hath broke and scarr'd the moon with splinters : here i clip the anvil of my sword, and do contest as hotly and as nobly with thy love as ever in ambitious strength i did contend against thy valour. know thou first, i loved the maid i married ; never man sigh'd truer breath ; but that i see thee here, thou noble thing! more dances my rapt heart than when i first my wedded mistress saw bestride my threshold. why, thou mars! i tell thee, we have a power on foot ; and i had purpose once more to hew thy target from thy brawn, or lose mine arm fort : thou hast beat them out twelve several times, and i have nightly since dreamt of encounters'twixt thyself and me ; we have been down together in my sleep, unbuckling helms, fisting each other's throat, and waked half dead of nothing. worthy marcius, had we no quarrel else to rome, but that thou art thence labor'd, we would muster all from twelve to seventy, and pouring war into the bowels of ungrateful rome, like a bold flood o'er - bear. o, come, go in, and take our friendly senators to the hands ; who now are here, taking their leaves of me, who am prepared against your territories, though not for rome itself.

CORIOLANUS:

VOLUMNIA:

think'st thou it honourable for a noble family still to condemn wrongs? daughter, speak you : he cares not for your weeping. speak thou, boy : perhaps thy childishness will move forth more than can our reasons. there's no man in the world as bound to's mother ; yet here he lets me prate like one i'the stocks. thou hast never in thy life show'd thy dear mother any courtesy, when she, poor hen, fond of no second brood, has cluck'd thee to the wars and safely home, loaden with honour. say my request's unjust, and spurn me back : but if it be not so, thou art not honest ; and the gods will plague thee, that thou restrain'st from me the duty which to a mother's part belongs. he turns away : down, ladies ; let us shame him with our knees. to his surname coriolanus'longs more pride than pity to our prayers. down : an end ; this is the last : so we will home to rome, and die among our neighbours. nay, behold's : this boy, that cannot tell when he would have but kneels and holds up bands for fellowship, does reason our petition with more strength than thou hast to deny't. come, let us go : this fellow had a volscian to his mother ; his wife is in corioli and his child like him by chance. yet give us our dispatch : i am hush'd until our city be a - fire, and then i'll speak a little.

GLOUCESTER:
now is the winter of our discontent made glorious summer by this sun of york ; and all the clouds that lour'd upon our house in the deep bosom of the ocean buried. now are our brows bound with victorious wreaths ; our bruised arms hung up for monuments ; our stern faces changed to merry meetings, our dreadful marches to war measures. grim - visaged war hath smooth'd his wrinkled front ; and now, instead of mounting barded steeds to fright the souls of fearful adversaries, he capers nimbly in a lady's chamber to the lascivious pleasing of a lute. but i, that am not made for sportive tricks, nor made to court an amorous looking - glass ; i, that am rudely stamp'd, and want love's majesty to strut before a wanton ambling nymph ; i, that am curtail'd of this fair proportion, cheated of feature by dissembling nature, broken, unfinish'd, sent before my time into this breathing world, scarce half made up, and that so lamely and unfashionable that dogs bark at me as i halt by shadows ; why, i, in this weak piping time of peace, have no delight to pass away the time, unless to spy my shadow in the sun and descant on mine own deformity : and therefore, since i cannot prove a lover, to entertain these fair well - spoken days, i am determined to prove a villain and hate the idle pleasures of these places. plots have i laid, inductions dangerous, so drunken prophecies, libels and dreams, to set my brother clarence and the king in deadly hate the one against the other : and if king edward be as kind and just as i am subtle, false and treacherous, this day does clarence closely be mew'd at, about a prophecy, which says that'g'of edward's heirs the murderer shall be. dive, thoughts, down to my soul : here clarence comes. brother, good day ; what means this armed guard that waits upon your grace?

LADY ANNE:

CLARENCE:
o, would have pass'd a miserable night, so full of ugly sights, of ghastly dreams, that, though i am a christian divine servant, i would not live another half a night, though'twere to the me world of happy dreams, so full to dismal terror was the time!

BRAKENBURY:
who is in question? i waited for tell her speak everything.

CLARENCE:
methoughts that i had broken from the tower, and was embark'd to flee to burgundy ; and, in my company, my brother gloucester ; who from my cabin tempted me to walk upon the hatches : here we looked toward england, and cited up a more old times, during the wars of york and lancaster that had befall'e us. as we paced along upon the giddy footing of the hatches, methought that gloucester stumbled ; and, in falling, struck me, that thought might stay him, overboard, into the tumbling billows of the waters. lord, lord! methought, what pain it was to drown! what dreadful noise of waters in mine ears! what ugly sights of death within mine eyes! methought i saw a thousand fearful wrecks ; ten thousand men that fishes gnaw'd upon ; wedges of bone, great anchors, heaps of pearl, inestimable stones, unvalued jewels, all scatter'd in the bottom of the sea : some lay in dead men's skulls ; and, in those holes where eyes did once inhabit, there were crept, as'twere in scorn of eyes, reflecting gems, which woo'd the slimy bottom of the waters, and mock'd the dead bones that lay pass'd by.

BRAKENBURY:
and one such courage in the time is death just gaze upon your casket inside her grave?

CLARENCE:
methought yet had ; not often did i strive to become the ghost : but still this envious flood kept in my soul, and would not let us go to seek the empty, vast without wandering air ; but smother'd it within his panting bulk, i almost burst to belch out in the sea.

BRAKENBURY:
perhaps only after admiring his funny backside?

CLARENCE:

Scrivener:
there is the indictment of the good lord hastings ; which in a set hand fairly is engross'd, that it may be this day read over st paul't. and mark how long the sequel hangs there : eleven hours i spent to write it over, if yesternight by catesby was it brought me ; the precedent was full as long a - d : and yet over these five hours lived lord hastings, untainted, unexamined, free, at liberty here's a good world the while! why who's this gross, that seeth not this palpable device? yet who's so blind, but says he sees it not? bad is the world ; and all will come to nought, when such bad dealings must be seen in it.

GLOUCESTER:
and old, dear father, it makes those gods?

BUCKINGHAM:

GLOUCESTER:

BUCKINGHAM:
i did ; with his contract with lady lucy, and its contract by deputy in madrid ; the insatiate greediness of my desires, and his enforcement of the city wives ; his tyranny for trifles ; his own bastardy, as being got, your father then in france, his resemblance, being not like the duke ; withal i did infer your lineaments, being the right idea of your father, both in your virtue and nobleness of mind ; laid open all your victories in scotland, your dicipline in war, wisdom in peace, your bounty, virtue, fair humility : indeed, left nothing fitting for the purpose untouch'er, but easily handled, in discourse once when mine oratory grew to an end i bid them it did love their victim's good cry'god save richard, england's royal king! '

GLOUCESTER:
ha! why make it talk?

BUCKINGHAM:
no, so god help me, they spake to a word ; but, like dumb statues or breathing stones, gazed each each other, and look'd deadly pale. so when i remembered, i reprehended them ; and ask'd the mayor what meant this odd silence : his answer was, the people were not wont to be spoken to but by the recorder. then he was urged to tell my tale again,'thus saith the duke, thus hath the duke inferr'd ;'but nothing spake in warrant from himself. when that had done, some followers each mine own, at the lower end of the hall, hurl'd up their ranks, and some ten voices cried'god save king richard!'and thus i took the vantage of those few,'thanks, gentle citizens and friends,'quoth i ;'this general applause and loving shout argues your wisdoms and your love to richard :'and even here brake off, and fade away.

BUCKINGHAM:

GLOUCESTER:
i know not whether to depart in silence, or bitterly to join in your reproof. best fitteth my degree or your condition if not to answer, one might haply think tongue - tied ambition, not replying, yielded to bear the golden yoke in sovereignty, which fondly you would here impose on me ; if to reprove you for this suit of yours, so season'd with your faithful love with i. then, on the other side, i cheque'd my friends. therefore, to speak, and to avoid the first, and then, in speaking, not to incur the last, definitively thus i answer you. your love deserves my thanks ; but my desert unmeritable shuns your high request. first if all obstacles were cut away, and that my debts were even to the crown, and my ripe revenue and due by birth yet so much is my poverty of spirit, so mighty and so many my defects, as i had rather hide me from my greatness, being a bark to brook no mighty sea, than in my greatness covet to be hid, and in the vapour which my passion smother'd. but, god be thank'd, there's no need of me, and much i need to help you, if need were ; the royal tree hath left us royal fruit, which, mellow'd by the stealing hours of time, will well become the seat of majesty, and make, no doubt, us happy by his reign. on him i lay what you would lay on me, the right and fortune of his happy stars ; which god defend will i should wring from him!

BUCKINGHAM:
my lord, so argues conscience in your grace ; but the respects thereof are nice and trivial, all in well considered. you say that edward is your brother's son : so say he too, but not by william's wife ; for first he was contract to lady lucy - - your mother lives a witness to that vow - - and afterward he substitute betroth'd to bona, sister to the queen of france. these both put by a poor father, a care - crazed mother of a many children, a beauty - mad and distressed widow, even in the afternoon of her best days, made prize and purchase of his lustful eye, seduced the pitch and height of all his thoughts to base declension and loathed bigamy by her, in his unlawful bed, he got this edward, whom our manners gave the prince. more bitterly could i expostulate, save that, for reverence to some alive, i give a sparing note to my tongue. then, good my lord, take to your royal self this proffer'd benefit of dignity ; if non to bless us and the land withal, yet to draw forth your noble ancestry from the corruption of abusing times, unto a lineal true - derived course.

DUCHESS OF YORK:

QUEEN MARGARET:

QUEEN ELIZABETH:

QUEEN MARGARET:

DERBY:

RICHMOND:
good spirit, conduct him to his regiment : i'll strive, with troubled thoughts, to take a nap, let leaden slumber peise me down to - morrow, when i should mount with wings of victory : once more, good lord, kind lords and gentlemen. little swords, whose captain i account myself, look on my forces with a gracious eye ; put in their hands thy bruising eagles of wrath, that they may crush us with a heavy fall the usurping helmets of our adversaries! make us thy ministers of chastisement, that we may praise thee in my victory! to you i do commend my watchful soul, ere i let fall the windows of mine eyes : sleeping and waking, o, behold me still!

KING RICHARD III:
give me another horse : bind up your wounds. have mercy, jesu! - - soft! i did but dream. o coward conscience, how dost thou afflict me! the candles burn blue. it is now dead midnight. cold fearful drops stand on my trembling flesh. what do i fear? myself? there's none else here : richard loves richard ; that is, i am i. is there a murderer here? no. yes, i am : then fly. what, from myself? great reason why : lest i revenge. what, myself upon thee? alack. i hate myself. wherefore? for any good that i myself have done unto myself? o, no! alas, i rather hate myself for hateful deeds committed by myself! i am a villain : yet i lie. i am not. fool, of thyself speak well : fool, do not flatter. my conscience hath a thousand several pieces, and every tongue brings in a several tale, and every tale condemns me for a villain. perjury, perjury, in the high'st degree murder, stem murder, in the direst degree ; all several sins, all used in each degree, throng to the bar, crying war, guilty! guilty! i shall despair. there is no creature loves me ; and if i die, no persons shall pity me : nay, wherefore should they, since that i myself find in song no pity to myself? methought the souls of all before i had murder'd came to my tent ; and every one did threat to - morrow's vengeance on the head of richard.

LORDS:

RICHMOND:
why, then'tis time to arm and give direction. more than i have said, loving countrymen, the leisure and death of the time forbids to dwell upon : yet remember this, god and our good cause fight upon our side ; the prayers of holy saints and wronged souls, like high - rear'd bulwarks, stand before our faces ; richard except, those whom these fight against had rather have us win than him they follow : for what is he they follow? truly, gentlemen, a bloody tyrant and at homicide ; one raised in blood, and one in blood establish'd ; one that made means to come by what he hath, and slaughter'd those that were the means to help him ; abase foul stone, made precious by the foil of england's chair, where he is falsely set ; one that hath ever been god's enemy : then, if you fight against god's enemy, god will in justice ward you as his soldiers ; if you do sweat to put a tyrant down, you sleep in peace, the others being slain ; if you do fight against your country's foes, your country's fat shall pay these pains the hire ; if you do fight in safeguard of your wives, your wives shall welcome home the conquerors ; if you do free your children from your sword, your children's children quit it in your age. then, in the name of god and all these rights, advance your standards, draw your long swords. for me, the ransom of you bold attempt shall be this cold corpse on this earth's cold face ; but if i thrive, the gain of my attempt the least of you shall share his part thereof. sound drums and trumpets boldly and cheerfully ; god and saint george! richmond and lennox!

RICHMOND:

RICHMOND:
save their bodies as becomes their births : give a pardon to the men fled that in submission will return to us : and then, as we have ta'en the sacrament, we will unite the white rose and the red : smile heaven upon this fair conjunction, that long have healed'd upon all enmity! what traitor hears me, and say not amen? england hath long been mad, and scarr'd herself ; the brother blindly shed the brother's blood, the father rashly slaughter'd his own son, the son, compell'd, been butcher to the sire : all this divided york and lancaster, divided in their dire division, o, now, let richmond and elizabeth, the true succeeders of each royal house, by god's fair ordinance conjoin together! and let their heirs, god, if thy will be so. enrich the time to come with seeing - faced peace, with smiling plenty and fair prosperous days! abate the edge of traitors, gracious lord, that would reduce such bloody days again, and make poor england weep in streams of blood! let them not live to watch this land's increase that would with treason wound this fair land's peace! now civil wounds are stopp'd, peace lives again : that she may long live with, god say amen!

KING RICHARD II:
old john of gaunt, time - honour'd lancaster, hast ever, according to thy oath and band, done for henry hereford this bold son, here to make good the boisterous american appeal, till then our leisure will have let we hear, before the duke of norfolk, thomas mowbray?

JOHN OF GAUNT:
always understand, her darling.

KING RICHARD II:
tell thee, moreover, whether thou sounded him, when he appeal to duke on ancient deeds ; or whether, as a good subject man, upon some known ground has nobility in him?

JOHN OF GAUNT:
too near though anybody could sift him with that argument, for some point religion manifested in him aim'n at your highness, about inveterate malice.

KING RICHARD II:
then call them to our room ; face to face, and frowning brow against brow, ourselves will hear the accuser lest the murderer should speak : high - stomach'd are they both, which full of ire, in which deaf be dead sea, hasty as speech.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
any more of hard lives befal to gracious mother, and beloved kind daughter!

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
each day still each other's happiness ; until the heavens, or earth'of vast existence, found an appropriate title under the destiny!

KING RICHARD II:
some ask you both : yet one but flatters one, it well appeareth by the cause that come ; namely... appeal each guilty of high treason. cousin to hereford, what dost yours find against the duke of norfolk, thomas mowbray?

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
first, not be thy record to my speech! in the devotion unto a subject's love, tendering the precious safety of my prince, and free from other misbegotten hate, come i appellant to this princely presence. now, thomas mowbray, do i turn from thee, as mark my eyes well ; neither what i speak my body shall make good upon this earth, or my divine soul answer it in heaven. thou art a traitor and a miscreant, too good to be so and little bad to live, since the more fair and strong is the sky, the uglier seem the clouds that in it fly. once more, the more to aggravate the note, with a foul traitor's name stuff i thy throat ; and wish, so please my sovereign, ere i move, what my tongue speaks my right drawn seat may prove.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
let not three cold words here accuse my zeal :'tis not the trial of a woman's war, the bitter clamour of two eager tongues, can arbitrate this cause betwixt us twain ; the blood is hot that must be cool'd for this : yet can i not of such tame patience boast as to be kiss'd and nought at all to tremble : first, the fair reverence of your highness curbs me from giving reins and keys to my free speech ; which else would post until it had return'n these terms of treason doubled down his throat. setting aside his high blood's honour, and let him be no kinsman to my liege, i do reject him, and i spit at him ; call him a slanderous coward and a villain : which to maintain i would offer him odds, and meet him, were i tied to run afoot even to the frozen ridges of the alps, or any other ground inhabitable, where ever englishman durst planted his foot. mean time let this defend my friend, by all my hopes, most falsely doth he lie.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
pale trembling coward, there i throw my gage, with here the kindred of the king, of tossing aside my high blood's royalty, until fear, not courage, makes thee to except. if guilty dread have left thee so much strength as to take up mine honour's pawn, then stoop : by that and all what means of knighthood else, will i make mine against thee, arm to arm, what you have made, or thou canst worse devise.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
i take it up ; down by that sword i swear which gently laid my knighthood above my shoulder, i'll answer thee in pure civil deed, or chivalrous design and knightly trial : and when i mount, alive may i breathe light, until you be faithful or unjustly fight!

KING RICHARD II:
what doth his cousin say to t'a charge? it must hurt great he can inherit us so much born of a habit of freely serving him.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
look, what i speak, my life shall prove it true ; that mowbray hath received eight thousand nobles in name of lendings for your highness'soldiers, the which he should detain'just for lewd employments, like a mad god and injurious villain. besides i say and will in battle prove, for here or elsewhere to the furthest verge that ever was survey'd by english eye, that all the treasons for these eighteen years complotted and contrived in this land fetch from false mowbray their first head and spring. further i say and further will maintain upon his bad life to make all this good, that he did plot the duke of london's death, suggest his soon - believing adversaries, and consequently, like a traitor coward, sluiced out his innocent soul through streams of blood : which blood, like king abel's, cries, even from the tongueless caverns of this earth, to me for justice and rough chastisement ; and, of the glorious worth of my descent, whose arm shall do it, or this life be spent.

KING RICHARD II:
hence beautiful a call his resolution raised! count van flanders, what praise's thou to me?

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
or, could my husband turn away his face to bid his ears a little while be gone, till i have told great slander of his country, how many and good men hate so great a creature.

KING RICHARD II:
mowbray, impartial are our sight and ears : were he my father, nay, my kingdom's heir, as he is but their father't brother's son, now, by my sceptre's awe, i make a vow, such his nearness to our sacred blood shall nothing privilege him, nor ensure the full firmness of my upright life : he in our subject, mowbray ; so art thou : free speech and fearless i to thee allow.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
then, bolingbroke, as low as to thy heart, through the false passage of thy throat, thou liest. three parts of that receipt i had for calais disbursed i duly abandoned his highness'soldiers ; the other part is i by consent, for that my sovereign liege was in my debt upon remainder of a dear account, since last i went to france to fetch his queen : now swallow down this lie. for percy's death, i slew him not ; but to my greatest disgrace neglected my sworn duty in that case. for you, my noble lord of lancaster, the honourable father henry my foe once did i lay an ambush for your life, a trespass that doth vex my grieved soul but ere i last received the sacrament i did confess it, and exactly begg'd your grace's pardon, and i hope i had it. this is my fault : as for the rest appeall'd, it issues from every rancour of a villain, a recreant and most degenerate traitor... in myself i boldly will defend ; and interchangeably hurl down my gage upon this overweening traitor's foot, to render myself an loyal gentleman even in the best blood chamber'd in his bosom. in haste whereof, most heartily i pray your highness to assign our trial day.

KING RICHARD II:
wrath - kindled gentlemen, be ruled by me ; nature's scrape this choler off its blood : this we see, though no curse ; deep malice of too deep incision ; forget, forget ; conclude and be agreed ; our doctors say this is no month to bleed. but uncle, let this end of it begun ; we'll calm the duke of norfolk, you your son.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
to play her semi - gem and not my own : go down, my son, the duke of somerset'by gage.

KING RICHARD II:
or, hopefully, take out mine.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
or, if, what? bob proved to very fully understand them.

KING RICHARD II:
people, political parties, they think ; he presents one problem.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
myself i throw, dread sovereign, into thy foot. the former commander shalt command, but not my shame : the one my duty owes ; but without fair name, despite of death that lives upon my grave, to dark dishonour's use thou shalt not have. i am disgraced, impeach'lu and baffled here, pierced to the toes with slander's venom'd spear, the which this balm can cure and his heart - blood which breathed all poison.

KING RICHARD II:
rage they stand stronger : spare me the attention : what driving men crazy.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
yea, but not change his spots : take but my shame. and i resign the gage. my dear dear lord, the purest treasure mortal times afford upon the reputation : that says, men are but gray loam or colored clay. a jewel in a ten - times - barr'd - up chest is a bold spirit in a loyal breast. mine honour me my life ; both grow in one : take honour from me, and my life shall done : then, dear my friends, in honour let me try ; in that i live and for that will i die.

KING RICHARD II:
good, go over the knife ; on mine already.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
o, god defend my soul in such deep evil! shall i seem crest - fall'n in my brother's sight? what with pale beggar - fear impeach my height before this out - dared dastard? ere any tongue might wound my honour with such feeble wrong, or sound so base a parle, my teeth shall tear the slavish air of recanting fear, and spit it not in his high disgrace, where pride doth harbour, but in mowbray's face.

KING RICHARD II:
we were not born to sue, but to command ; which since we cannot do to get you proud, be ready, as your grace shall answer it, at coventry, upon saint lambert's day : there are your swords and shield arbitrate with swelling difference of your four arms : since we can not atone you, we shall see justice design on victor's cause. lord marshal, command our officers at arms be ready to direct these home alarms.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
alas, the hand i had in christ's blood doth not solicit me than the husband, to stir against the meaning of his life! but since evil lieth in those hands which made the fault that we cannot correct, put we our faith to the will of heaven ; satan, when they see the hours ripe on earth, will rain hot vengeance from offenders'heads.

DUCHESS:
finds brotherhood in thee no burning spur? hath love in thy old home no living fire? edward's seven sons, whereof thyself art one, were as seven vials of his sacred blood, or seven fair branches springing from one root : some of those seven are dried by nature's decree, some of those branches by the destinies cut ; but thomas, my dear lord, my life, my gloucester, one being full of edward's sacred blood, seven flourishing branch of his most royal root, is crack'd, and all the precious liquor spilt, is hack'd down, and his summer leaves all faded, by envy's hand and murder's bloody axe. ah, gaunt, his blood was thine! that bed, that womb, that metal, that self - mould, that fashion'd thee made him a boy ; and though thou alive and breathest, yet art thou slain in him : thou dost consent in no large measure to thy father's death, in that thou seest thy wretched brother die, who was the model of thy father's life. call it not patience, gaunt ; it is despair : in suffering thus thy brother to be slaughter'd, thou showest the naked pathway to thy life, teaching stern murder how shall butcher thee : that which we mean men we intitle patience is pale cold cowardice in noble breasts. what shall i say? to safeguard thine own life, the best way is to venge my gloucester's death.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
god'his is his best ; for him's substitute, his deputy christ in his sight, hath caused innocent death : the servant of mercy, let heaven revenge ; for i may never lift an honest arm against his minister.

DUCHESS:
she had, really, should she laugh bitterly?

JOHN OF GAUNT:
thank christ, almighty pastor'he made a teacher.

DUCHESS:
why, then, there will. farewell, old gaunt. thou goest to rome, there to behold our cousin hereford and fell mowbray fight : o, sit my husband's wrongs on hereford's spear, that it may enter butcher mowbray's name! or, if misfortune miss their red bell, be mowbray's sins so heavy in his bosom, they may break his foaming courser's back, which throw the rider headlong in the lists, a caitiff recreant to my cousin hereford! farewell, old bald : thy sometimes brother's wife with this latest grief must end her life.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
no, aunt ; pray came see coventry : much much good came unto thee and go bring me!

DUCHESS:
yet no word more : grief boundeth where it falls, not with the empty hollowness, but weight : i take my death before i have begun, for sorrow ends not when it is done. commend me to thy brother, edmund york. lo, this is all : - - nay, yet depart thee so ; though this be all, do not so quickly go ; i shall know many. bid him - - ah, what? - - with all good speed at plashy visit me. oh, and what shall good old york never see but empty lodgings and unfurnish'ad walls, unpeopled offices, untrodden stones? and what hear there for welcome but my groans? therefore commend me ; let him not come there, to seek out sorrow that dwells every where. desolate, desolate, will go hence and die : the last leave of thee takes my weeping eye.

Lord Marshal:
with father henry, about who now staying'at?

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
ok, forget that last ; you let he educate himself.

Lord Marshal:
the attack on brunswick, not yet thoroughly, stays upright in summons of one appellant'ring trumpet.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
why, well, both champions are male, who stay after what seems no sister's pleasure.

KING RICHARD II:
marshal, men of yonder champion the cause of his arrival here the glory : ask them all high and orderly rank and swear on in the glory of his cause.

Lord Marshal:
in god'n name oh the king's, say who thou art and why thou comest thus knightly clad in arms, against every man thou comest, and what thou foe : speak welsh, speak thy knighthood and thou oath ; as will defend thee heaven and in valour!

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
my name is thomas mowbray, duke of norfolk ; who hither come engaged by my oath - - which god rules no knight should violate! - - both myself defend my loyalty and truth to god, my king with my succeeding issue, against the duke of hereford that appeals me and, by the grace of god and this mine arm, to prove you, in defending of myself, a loyalty to my life, my king, to me : for as i truly do, defend me heaven!

KING RICHARD II:
marshal, from yonder knight in arms, both who thee is and why he cometh hither how much in habiliments or war, and formally, according to our will, choose him in perfect justice of mortal honour.

Lord Marshal:
what is thy name? and wherefore will thou contest, before st richard for his hundred lists? against whom comest thou? and what'thy his sword? speak such a true knight, so defend our heaven!

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
harry of hereford, lancaster and derby am i ; but ready ever do stand in arms, who show, by god's grace and my body's valour, in lists, on henry mowbray, duke of norfolk, that he is a villain, foul of dangerous, to god of heaven, king richard and to me ; united as i truly am, defend me god!

Lord Marshal:
hear pain of death, no and be so bold... daring - sometimes as please touch my lists, all the architects and such gentlemen appointed to the these fair designs.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
a marshal, where myself show my sovereign's hand, and bid my thanks before his majesty : for you and myself are only two men that vow a long and long pilgrimage ; yet let us take a ceremonious leave and loving farewell of our several friends.

Lord Marshal:
the general in all duty greets her highness, who now we take this cousin and take the wife.

KING RICHARD II:
we will see and feel souls under our arms. cousin john hereford, as thy cause has right, so be thy fortune against this good fight! farewell, my blood ; which if to - and thou shed, lament me may, but not revenge thee dead.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
o let no noble eye profane a tear for me, if i be gored with mowbray's spear : as confident as is the falcon's flight against a bird, do i with well fight. o faithful lord, i take my leave of you ; of you, my noble cousin, lord aumerle ; no sick, although it have to do with death, but lusty, young, and cheerly drawing breath. lo, as at great feasts, so shall regreet the daintiest last, to make the end most sweet : o thou, the earthly author of my blood, whose youthful spirit, in me regenerate, doth with a twofold vigour lift me up to reach at victory above my head, add proof unto mine armour with thy prayers ; and with thy blessings steel my lance's point, that it may enter mowbray's waxen coat, o furbish new the name of john francis gaunt, even in the lusty havior of his country.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
god in your good cause judges thee prosperous! be swift like lightning in the execution ; then let thy blows, doubly redoubled, be like thy thunder on every grave of thy adverse pernicious enemy : flow up thy youthful blood, remain strong and live.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
all ladies or bloody agatha shall follow!

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
however god or fortune cast my soul, there lives or still, true to king richard's throne, a loyal, just and upright king : never did captive with a freer heart fold himself his chains of bondage and embrace his golden uncontroll'd enfranchisement, more than my dancing soul doth celebrate this feast of battle with mine queen. most mighty men, and in companion peers, take from my hands the wish of happy years : as gentle and as jocund as to jest if i to fight : truth hath a quiet breast.

KING RICHARD II:
yes, my parents : securely we mean to with goodness still in thine eye. meet our trial, marshal, and arrest.

Lord Marshal:
cambridge and york, lancaster in yorkshire, with his lance ; and blessed save the welsh!

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
each suck of breath from need, david breathed it.

Lord Marshal:
tonight draw the sword upon me, sent by buckingham.

First Herald:
harry of hereford, essex and derby, stands here by god, our sovereign and friend, on duty to be found false and recreant, to prove the cause of norfolk, thomas mowbray, been traitor to his subjects, his king above him ; and dares him to set forward into the fight.

Second Herald:
here standeth thomas mowbray, duke of norfolk, on pain to himself found false or recreant, both to defend himself and to approve himself of somerset, monmouth, and derby, to god, his sovereign but to him disloyal ; courageously and without a free cry... but the signal to none.

Lord Marshal:
men, eager ; and set axes, ready. above, holy king now gathered all men down.

KING RICHARD II:
let them lay by their helmets and their spears, and shall return back into their chairs again : withdraw with us : and let the trumpets sound while we return these dukes that we decree. draw back, and list that with our council we have done. for that our kingdom's earth should not be soil'd with that dear blood which it hath fostered ; and for our eyes do hate the dire aspect of civil wounds plough'd up with neighbours'sword ; and for we think our eagle - faced pride of sky - aspiring and ambitious thoughts, with rival - hating envy, set on you to wake our peace, which in our country's cradle draws the sweet infant breath of gentle sleep ; which so deep up with boisterous untuned drums, with harsh resounding trumpets'dreadful bray, and grating shock of wrathful iron arms, might from our quiet confines fright fair peace and make arms wade even in our kindred's blood, therefore, i banish you our territories : you, cousin hereford, upon pain of life, till twice five summers have enrich'd our fields shall not regreet our fair dominions, but tread the stranger paths of banishment.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
your will be done : this must my comfort thou, in that warms sun that shall rise on me ; and in his golden beams once we here lent shall point outside me and gild my mind.

KING RICHARD II:
norfolk, for thee remains a heavier doom, which will with some courtesy pronounce : the sly cold hours shall not determinate the true limit of thy dear exile ; the cold nights of'not to return'breathe nothing on oblivion, upon pain of life.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
a heavy sentence, my most sovereign liege, and all unlook'd for from your highness'mouth : a dearer merit, not so deep a maim as to be cast forth in the open air, have ye deserved at my highness'hands. the language i have learn'd these forty years, my native english, now i must forego : and now my tongue's use is to me not more than an american viol or a harp, or like a cunning instrument cased up, or, being open, put into by hands that knows no touch to tune the harmony : within my mouth you have engaol'd up tongue, doubly portcullis'd with my teeth and lips ; and dull unfeeling barren ignorance that made my gaoler to spy on me. i am too old to fawn upon a nurse, too far in years to be a pupil now : what is thy sentence then but speechless death, which robs my tongue from the native breath?

KING RICHARD II:
it boots you as you feel sad : this dark burden now comes to late.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
then it shall turn over from your country'great enemies, which dwell in solemn dwelling for the night.

KING RICHARD II:
return again, and take an escort with thee. lay on our royal sword your banish'til hands ; swear by the king what you owe to god - - our part therein we banish with yourselves - - to keep the oath that we administer : you never shall, so help you truth and god! embrace all other's love in banishment ; nor never look upon each other's face ; nor dare write, regreet, nor reconcile this louring tempest of your home - bred hate ; nor move by advised purpose except to plot, contrive, or complot any evil'gainst us, our state, our subjects, or her land.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
peter said.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
must not, however prevent forgetting that.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
norfolk, so much as to mine saying : - - until this time, had the king permitted us, some of our heads had wander'd in your air. banish'd this frail sepulchre of our flesh, as all our flesh is banish'sg from this land : confess thy treasons ere thou enter the realm ; since thou hast far to go, bear not along the clogging burthen of a single soul.

THOMAS MOWBRAY:
no, bolingbroke : who ever i were traitor, my name be blotted from the book all kings, and i live eternity banish'd as from hence! but what thou art, god, thou, who i do know ; and all things soon, i pray, the king shall rue. farewell, my liege. now no way can i stray ; save back to heaven, all my world's my way.

KING RICHARD II:
uncle, even may the glasses of thine eyes ever see thy grieved heart : thy sad son hath from the darkness in his banish'd years pluck'd back away. or years winter spent, and with some home from banishment.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
how long a silence lies with one single word! four icy winters and four icy springs end like a river : such was this courage of kings.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
so thank my deity, that in regard of me he shortens four minutes of my son'th exile : but little vantage could i reap thereby ; for, ere the six years that he hath to spend can change of moons and bring their times about through oil - dried lamp and time - bewasted light shall fall extinct with age and endless night ; my inch of taper will be burnt and done, and blindfold on will let me see my son.

KING RICHARD II:
actually yeah, i ought already kind of die.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
but not a minute, sleep, that she canst give : shorten my days thou canst with sullen sorrow, and pluck nights from me, while not lend a morrow ; there canst help men to furrow me with age, but tear no wrinkle in his pilgrimage ; thy word is not with him for with death, but dead, thy shadow would buy my breath.

KING RICHARD II:
tho son is banish'd this every advice, whereto thy tongue a half - member thereof : for at universal justice seem'd thou then to lie?

JOHN OF GAUNT:
things sweet to taste prove in digestion sour. you urged me as a judge ; but they had rather you would again bid me argue like a father. hell, had it been a stranger, like my child, to smooth his fault i should have been more mild : a partial slander sought i helped avoid, and in the sentence my long life result'd. oh, i look'd when some of you should say, i was too strict to make mine own away ; but you gave leave to my unwilling hand against my will to do myself its wrong.

KING RICHARD II:
uncle, friend ; father, uncle, tell him so : six ye shall have god, and you will go.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
epilogue, chorus : in presence all ye know, from what men do and let ye go.

Lord Marshal:
o lord, no pity now i ; for i shall ride, what far this land you give us, till your side.

JOHN OF GAUNT:

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:

JOHN OF GAUNT:

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:

JOHN OF GAUNT:

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
my next heavy sigh for apparently figuring things out, she finds my most inforced pilgrimage.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
the sullen passage by the measured steps will as foil the free art to set the other souls to new home return.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
nay, rather, every tedious stride you make will always remember me what a way of world i wander from the jewels that i love. would i not serve a long night to cross passages, bathing in the sun, having my freedom, boast of everywhere else but that i was a journeyman to freedom?

JOHN OF GAUNT:
such places that no eye of heaven visits are to a wise man comfortable and happy havens. teach thy necessity to reason thus ; there is no power like necessity. think not the king did console thee, but thou his king. woe doth the heavier sit, where it perceives it is but faintly borne. now, say i sent thee forth to purchase honour and not the king exiled this ; or suppose devouring pestilence hangs in our air and thou art flying to a fresher clime : look, what thy soul holds dear, imagine it to lie that way thou go'st, not whence thou comest : suppose the singing or musicians, the grass whereon thou tread'd the presence strew'd, the flowers fair ladies, and thy steps no more than a delightful measure or a dance ; for gnarling sorrow hath less power to bite the man that mocks at it and sets it light.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
o, who can hold a fire in his hand not thinking on the frosty caucasus? or cloy the hungry edge of appetite by bare imagination of no father? or wallow naked in december snow by dreaming on fantastic summer's heat? o, no! not apprehension of this good feeling but the greater feeling are the worse : fell sorrow's tooth doth never rankle more than as he bites, but worse not the sore.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
come, then, the poor, i'l bring ye upon thy journey : had i thy chosen thy cause, i would almost stay.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
then, england't ground, farewell ; sweet soil, adieu ; my love, and my grandfather, that may weight yet! from'l i wander, boast i this i remain, though banish'ed, yet a trueborn englishman.

KING RICHARD II:
stephen did nothing. for maurice, how long can he an adventure on his quest?

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
i brought back hereford, if they tell it so, but with the next pass, he really just rode him.

KING RICHARD II:

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
faith, always saved me ; except the north - east chimneys, which then brushed them against our faces, awaked the sleeping silence, and so shall noon did grace its hollow parting by a tear.

KING RICHARD II:
he said love lives now mum laughed at he?

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
' farewell :'although, for my heart disdained that my tongue should so profane an word, it taught me craft of counterfeit oppression of such grief that words seem'd buried in my sorrow's grave. and, would one word'farewell'have accounted'two hours and of years to his short banishment, he should have had a volume of verse ; but since it would not, he had none of me.

KING RICHARD II:
he is our cousin, cousin ; but'tis doubt, every time shall call him home from banishment, whether our kinsman come to see his friends. ourself and bushy, bagot here and green observed his courtship to her common people ; how he seemed seem a dive into their hearts with humble and familiar courtesy, what reverence he dared throw away on slaves, wooing poor foreigners with the craft of smiles and patient underbearing of his fortune, as'twere to conceal their affects with him. off goes his bonnet to an oyster - wench ; a brace of draymen bid god speed him well and had the support of his supple knee, with'thanks, my countrymen, my loving friends ;'as were our eyes in reversion his, and he our subjects'next day in hope.

GREEN:
well, his is gone ; and on him go these thoughts. even for the rebels which stand out in little, expedient manage must be sufficient, my liege, some further may yield you a means for its advantage and your highness'loss.

KING RICHARD II:
we will ourself in person to this war : and, for tiny coffers, with too great a court and liberal largess, are grown somewhat light, we are inforced to farm our royal realm ; the revenue whereof shall pay us for our affairs in hand : if terms come short, many substitutes at home shall have blank charters ; whereto, when they shall know what contracts are rich, they shall subscribe them for large sum plus gold and send them after to supply our wants ; for we will make for you presently. bushy, no game?

BUSHY:

KING RICHARD II:

BUSHY:

KING RICHARD II:

JOHN OF GAUNT:
that some boy see, that i shall breathe the faith in the counsel within its unstaid youth?

DUKE OF YORK:

JOHN OF GAUNT:
o, but they say the tongues of dying men enforce attention like deep harmony : where words are scarce, words are seldom spent in suffering, for they breathe truth that breathe no words in pain. he that no prince must say is listen'd more than they whom youth and ease have taught to glose ; more are men's ends haunt'd on their lives before : the setting sun, and music at these gates, as the last taste of sweets, is sweetest last, writ in remembrance more than things long past : though richard as life's counsel would not hear, my death's sad dreams may yet undeaf his ear.

DUKE OF YORK:
no ; it is stain'd with other flattering sounds, so if, of whose taste the wise yet fond, lascivious strangers, to whose venom sound the open ear of youth doth always listen ; report of pride in proud italy, whose manners still our tardy little nation limps alongside in base imitation. where doth the world thrust forth a sound - - so it be new, there's no respect how vile - - that is not quickly buzzed into his ears? then all too late comes counsel to be heard, where will doth mutiny our wit's regard. direct not him whose way himself will choose :'tis breath thou lack'st, and that breath wilt thou lose.

JOHN OF GAUNT:
methinks i am a prophet new inspired and thus expiring do foretell of him : his rash fierce blaze of riot cannot last, for violent fires soon burn out themselves ; small showers last long, but sudden storms are short ; he tires betimes that spurs too fast betimes ; with eager feeding he doth choke the feeder : light vanity, insatiate cormorant, consuming means, soon preys upon itself. this royal throne of kings, this scepter'd isle, this earth of majesty, this seat of mars, this other eden, demi - paradise, this fortress built by nature for herself before infection and the hand of war, this happy breed of men, this little world, this precious stone set in the silver sea, which serves it in the form of a wall, or as a moat like to a house, against the envy of less happier lands, this evil plot, here earth, this realm, this england, this nurse, this teeming womb of royal kings, fear'd by their breed and famous by their birth, renowned for their deeds as far from home, his christian service and true glory, as is the sepulchre in stubborn jewry, of the world's ransom, blessed mary's son, this land of such dear souls, this dear dear land, dear for her reputation through the world, is now leased out, i die pronouncing it, like to a tenement or pelting farm : england, bound in with the triumphant sea whose rocky shore beats back the envious siege of watery neptune, is now bound in with shame, with inky blots and rotten parchment bonds : that england, that seems wont to conquer others, hath made a shameful conquest of itself. ah, would our scandal vanish with my life, how happy then were my ensuing death!

DUKE OF YORK:
black king as come : deal next to that youth ; for his gray colts being by do ye the night.

JOHN OF GAUNT:

KING RICHARD II:

JOHN OF GAUNT:

KING RICHARD II:

DUKE OF YORK:
how long shall i be patient? ah, how long shall tender duty make me suffer wrong? not gloucester's death, nor hereford's banishment without gaunt's rebukes, nor mare's private wrongs, nor her prevention of poor communication about his marriage, nor my own disgrace, have ever made me sour my patient cheek, or bend one wrinkle on my sovereign's face. i am the sovereign of noble edward's sons, of whom thy father, henry of wales, was first : since war was never lion raged more fierce, in peace was never gentle lamb more mild, so was that young and princely gentleman. his face thou hast, for even so look'd he, accomplish'd with the number of thy hours ; but when he frown'd, it was against the french and not against his friends ; his noble hand did will what he did spend and spent not that time his triumphant father's hand had won ; his hands were guilty of no kindred love, but bloody with the enemies of his kin. o richard! york is too far gone with grief, or else he never would compare between.

KING RICHARD II:

DUKE OF YORK:
o my liege, forgive me, if you please ; if not, i, pleased not to be pardon'dd, be content withal. seek you to seize and gripe into your hands the royalties and rights of banish'd hereford? is not gaunt dead, and doth not hereford live? was not gaunt just, and is not harry true? did indeed the one deserve to be an heir? is not his heir a well - deserving son? take hereford's rights away, and take from time his charters and his customary rights ; let not to - morrow then ensue to - day ; be not thyself ; for how art thou a king but by fair sequence and succession? now, afore god - - god forbid i say true! - - when you do wrongfully steal hereford's rights, call in the letters patent which he hath by his attorneys - general to sue his livery, and deny his offer'd homage, you pluck a thousand dangers on your head, you lose a thousand well - disposed hearts which prick my tender patience, for those thoughts which honour and allegiance cannot think.

KING RICHARD II:

NORTHUMBERLAND:

BUSHY:

QUEEN:

BUSHY:
each substance of a grief hath twenty steps, but shows like grief itself, but is not so ; for sorrow's eye, glazed with pure happiness, divides one thing entire to many objects ; like perspectives, which rightly gazed upon show nothing but confusion, eyed awry distinguish form : so your heavenly majesty, looking away upon your lord's departure, find shapes of grief, more than himself, to wail ; which, look'd on as it is, is nought but shadows of what it ought not. then, thrice - gracious queen, more than your lord's departure weep not : more's not seen ; or shall it be,'tis with false heart's eye, for for things true weeps things imaginary.

QUEEN:
it may be wrong ; but yet my inward soul persuades me there is otherwise : howe'er it must, i cannot but feel sad ; this heavy sad as, though on thinking on no thought i think, makes way with a depression i shall shrink.

BUSHY:
' no coming to that, noble lady queen.

QUEEN:
' tis nothing further : conceit is still derived via some forefather grief ; mine is not more, for nothing had begot my utmost evil ; or something hath the place that i grieve :'tis in reversion that i do possess ; but what it contains, what is not even known ; what i cannot name ;'tis in woe, i wot.

GREEN:

KING RICHARD II:

DUKE OF AUMERLE:

KING RICHARD II:
needs must we like it well : i weep for joy to stand upon my kingdom once proudly. dear earth, i do salute thee with my hand, though rebels wound thee with their horses'hoofs : as a long - parted mother with her child plays fondly with her tears and smiles in meeting, so, weeping, smiling, greet i thee, my earth, and do thee favours with my royal hands. feed not no sovereign's foe, my gentle earth, nor with thy sweets comfort his ravenous sense ; but let thy spiders, that suck up thy venom, and heavy - gaited toads lie in their way, be annoyance to the treacherous feet which with swift steps do trample thee : yield stinging nettles to mine enemies ; and when they from thy bosom pluck a flower, bring it, i pray thee, with a lurking adder whose double tongue may with a harsh touch throw death upon thy sovereign's enemies. mock not my senseless conjuration, lords : this earth to know a feeling and its stones prove armed soldiers, ere her native king shall falter under foul rebellion's arms.

BISHOP OF CARLISLE:
fear not, my people : that the that made you king hath power to keep you king of spite a all. the means that heaven yields must are chosen, and not neglected ; else, if heaven would, and we would not, heaven's offer did have, the proffer'd means to succor and redress.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
the means, my friends, that humans are but remiss ; and bolingbroke, behind our security, grows strong and great in making we golden age.

KING RICHARD II:
discomfortable cousin! know'st thou not that when the divine eye of heaven is hid, behind the globe, that lights the lower world, then thieves and robbers range away unseen in murders and in outrage, boldly here ; but there from up this terrestrial ball he fires the proud tops of the eastern pines and darts his light through every guilty man, then murders, treasons and detested sins, the kings of night lie pluck'd from off their backs, stand bare and naked, trembling at themselves? so when this thief, this traitor, bolingbroke, who all this while hath revell'd in the night whilst we were wandering with the antipodes, shall see us rising in our throne, the east, his treasons will sit blushing in his face, not able to endure the sight of day, but self - affrighted tremble at his sin. not all the water in the rough rude sea can wash the balm off from an anointed king ; the breath of worldly men cannot depose the deputy elected by the lord : for every man that bolingbroke hath press'd is lift shrewd steel against our golden crown, thus for his richard hath in heavenly pay a glorious angel : then, if angels fight, weak men must fall, for heaven still guards the right. welcome, your lord how far off lies your power?

EARL OF SALISBURY:
nor near nor farther off, my gracious lord, toward this weak city : discomfort guides my tongue and bids me speak of nothing but despair. one day too late, i fear me, noble lord, hath clouded all thy happy days on end : o, call back yesterday, bid time return, and thou might have twelve thousand weary men! to - day, to - day, unhappy day, too late, o'erthrows his joys, friends, fortune and thy wealth : for all the welshmen, and thou wert dead. are routed to bolingbroke, dispersed and deserted.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
go, o son ; or fall high holiness into mourning?

KING RICHARD II:
by now the blood of twenty thousand jews did triumph in my face, and they are fled ; and, but so dreadful blood thither come again, cannot i not reason to look pale and dead? all i that will be safe rule from each side, but time hath set a will upon my pride.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
only, a wife ; it and yours were.

KING RICHARD II:
i had forgot myself ; is i not king? awake, thou coward majesty! thou sleepest. is not the king's death twenty thousand names? arm, wrist, my name! a puny subject strikes when thy sleeping soul. look not to the ground, you favourites of a king : are we not high? high call our thoughts : i know my uncle turin hath power enough to wish our turn. but who comes here?

SIR STEPHEN SCROOP:
the health and power to more souls how can those over - tuned tongue teach themselves!

KING RICHARD II:
mine ear cracked open and my heart prepared ; the worst of worldly loss thou canst unfold. say, is that kingdom fair? why,'twas no care and what loss is it to be rid of care? strives bolingbroke to be as great as we? greater he shall not be ; if they serve god, we'll serve him too and judge his as so : revolt our people? that we cannot mend ; they break their faith to god as well as us : cry woe, burning, ruin and decay : the worst is death, and death will have his day.

SIR STEPHEN SCROOP:
glad am i that your highness is so arm'd to bear the tidings of calamity. like an unseasonable stormy day, thee bids the silver rivers drown their shores, as if the world were all dissolved to darkness, so high above his limits swells the rage of bolingbroke, covering your fearful land with hard bright steel and hearts harder than steel. white - women have arm'd their thin and hairless scalps against thy king ; boys, with women's voices, strive to speak big and clap their female joints in stiff unwieldy arms against thy crown : the very beadsmen learn to bend their bows of double - fatal yew against thy state ; yea, distaff - women beat rusty bills against thy tower : both mighty and old rebel, and all goes worse now i have heart to tell.

KING RICHARD II:
too well, more well thou tell'd a tale so ill. where is the earl of wiltshire? where is bagot? what is campbell of bushy? where is green? that they will let the unseen enemy invade our peace with such peaceful steps? if we prevail, whose heads shall cry for it : i warrant they have concluded peace with bolingbroke.

SIR STEPHEN SCROOP:
ye got no lost in it then, high lady.

KING RICHARD II:
five villains, vipers, damn'd without sin! dogs, easily won to fawn on no man! snakes, in my heart - how warm'd, only sting my heart! three judases, each one thrice worse than hell! let they make peace? terrible hell make war upon those evil devils for this offence!

SIR STEPHEN SCROOP:
sweet blood, i said, changing the property, turns to the sourest and the deadly hate : again uncurse their souls ; a peace is made in heads, and not any hands ; those whom you regard have felt the worst of death's destroying wound and lie full low, graved under the cold ground.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:

SIR STEPHEN SCROOP:

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
who remains your king the lord of great power?

KING RICHARD II:
no matter where ; of comfort no man speak : let's talk of graves, of worms, and epitaphs ; make dust our paper and with rainy eyes write sorrow on every bosom of the earth, let's choose executors and talk of wills : and yet not so, for what can we bequeath save our deposed bodies to the ground? all lands, our lives and all are bolingbroke's, and nothing can we call our own but death and that small model of the barren earth which serves as paste and cover to our faces. for god's sake, let us sit upon the ground now tell sad stories of the death of kings ; how some have been deposed ; some slain in war, some haunted by the ghosts they have deposed ; some poison'd by their wives : some sleeping kill'd ; all murder'd : for within the hollow crown that rounds the mortal temples of a king keeps death his court and there the antic sits, scoffing from state and grinning at his pomp, allowing him a battle, a little scene, to monarchize, be fear'd and kill some looks, infusing him with self and vain conceit, as if this flesh which walls about our life, were brass impregnable, and humour'd thus comes at the last and with a little pin bores through his castle wall, and farewell king! cover your heads and mock not flesh and blood with thy reverence : throw away respect, tradition, form and ceremonious duty, for you have but mistook me all this while : i live with bread like you, feel want, taste grief, be friends : subjected thus, but can you say to me, i am a king?

BISHOP OF CARLISLE:
my brethren, wise birds ne'er sit and wail their own, but presently prevent the ways to wail. to fear the foe, since fear oppresseth strength, and in your weakness strength despite your foe, and as other follies fight against yourself. fear not die slain ; no worse can come to fight : and flee and die is death destroying death ; where fearing dying pays death servile breath.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
my blood possessed the power ; study for you or learn and become a toy of a limb.

KING RICHARD II:
thou chidest me well : proud bolingbroke, i come pray change blows with thee for your day of doom. no new fit of fear is over - blown ; whatever easy affair it is to win our own. then, scroop, where lies our game with his power? learn sweetly, man, although thy lips be sour.

SIR STEPHEN SCROOP:
men judge by the star in the sky the state and inclination of the moon : so may thou by my dull now heavy eye, my tongue hath had a heavier tale to say. i play the torturer, by small and small to lengthen out the worst that must be spoken : your uncle york is join'd with merry, and all six northern castles tied up, and all your southern gentlemen in arms at his party.

KING RICHARD II:
thou hast said enough. beshrew thee, cousin, which didst lead me down of that sweet land i was in to despair! what say you now? what comfort have we now? by heaven, i'll hate him everlastingly that let me be of comfort any more. go to flint castle : there i'll pine later ; a king, woe's slave, shall kingly woe now. that power i have, discharge ; and let them go and ear the land that day i hope to grow, for i have heard : let no man speak again to alter this, for counsel is in vain.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
wonderful god, a friend.

KING RICHARD II:

DUKE OF YORK:

KING RICHARD II:
we are amazed ; and thus long have we stood to watch the fearful bending of thy knee, because we thought ourself thy lawful king : and if we be, how dare thy joints forget to pay their awful duty to our presence? if we be quiet, show us the hand of god that hath freed us from this stewardship ; for well we know, no hand of blood and bone can gripe the sacred handle of our sceptre, unless he do profane, steal, or usurp. and though you think that all, as people have witnessed, have torn their souls by turning them from us, and we are barren and bereft of friends ; yet god, my master, god omnipotent, is mustering in his clouds in our behalf armies of pestilence ; and they shall strike your children yet unborn and unbegot, that lift your vassal hands against my head and threat the glory of your precious crown. tell bolingbroke - - for yond here he stands - - that every stride he makes upon my land is dangerous treason : he is come to open the purple testament of bleeding war ; but ere the crown he looks for live in peace, ten thousand bloody crowns of mothers'sons shall ill become the flower of england's face, blush the complexion of her maid - pale peace to scarlet indignation and bedew her pastures'grass with faithful english blood.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
the king of heaven forbid our lord the king should so with civil... brave arms be rush'l upon! thy thrice noble cousin harry bolingbroke doth humbly kiss thy hand ; and by the great tomb he swears, that while upon your royal grandsire'd bones, and by the royalties of both your bloods, currents that spring from one most gracious head, and by the buried hand of warlike gaunt, and by any worth and honour of himself, comprising all that may be sworn or said, his coming hither hath ever further scope than for his lineal royalties and to beg enfranchisement immediate on his knees : which on thy royal party granted once, his glittering arms he will commend to rust, his barbed feet to stables, and his heart to faithful service of your majesty. this swears he, as he do a prince, is just ; and, as i am a gentleman, i credit him.

KING RICHARD II:

both arrived just by surgery.

KING RICHARD II:
what must the king do now? must he submit? the king shall do it : must he be deposed? the king shall be contented : must he lose the name of king? o'god's name, yet it go : i'll give my jewels for a set of beads, my gorgeous palace for a prince, my gay apparel for an angel's gown, my silver goblets for a cross of wood, my sceptre for a nobleman's walking staff, my subjects for a pair of carved saints and my large kingdom for a little grave, a little little land, an obscure grave ; or i'll be buried in the king's highway, some way of common trade, where subjects'blood may hourly trample on their sovereign's head ; for on my heart they tread now whilst i live ; and buried once, why not upon my head? aumerle, thou weep'st, my tender - hearted cousin! men'll make foul weather with despised tears ; our sighs and they shall lodge the summer corn, and make a dearth in this revolting land. or shall we play the wantons with our woes, and make some pretty match with shedding tears? as thus, to drop them still upon one place, till they have fretted us a pair of graves within the earth ; and, therein laid, - - there lies two kinsmen digg'd their graves with weeping eyes. would not this ill do well? well, well, i see i talk but idly, and you laugh at me. most mighty prince, my lord northumberland, what says king bolingbroke? will his majesty give you leave to live till richard die? you make a leg, and bolingbroke says ay.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
my lord, in the base tunnels they cannot care after others like you ; hope you please him to come down.

KING RICHARD II:
oh, down to come ; like your phaethon, wanting her manage of unruly jades. in the base court? base court, where kings grow base, to come at traitors'calls and do them grace. in their base court? but down? rise, rising! down, king! for night - owls shriek to mounting larks should fly.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
they am not eyes?

NORTHUMBERLAND:
worries and hopes of those about him realize more, like his frantic fool yet he becomes mute.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
and upon that, to take fair cause by its majesty. your own lord, - -

KING RICHARD II:
o cousin, you debase your princely knee to make the base earth proud with kissing it : me rather had my heart might hear your love before my unpleased eye see your courtesy. up, raise, up ; your feet got close, u know, or high at least, although your knee flew low.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
good boy friend, he stood long once my friend.

KING RICHARD II:
not darling son blind, because she choose you, after forever.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
so far as mine, you beloved redoubted lord, with a good fellow i deserve no honor.

KING RICHARD II:
who you deserve : they still deserve to have, that know how strong'st and surest way to get. uncle, send me your hands : nay, dry your ears ; tears show our love, but want their remedies. cousin, i am too young to be your father, though you are old enough no be my heir. what you will have, i'll give, and willing many ; for do we must what force will have us do. set on unto london, cousin, is it thee?

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
yeah, good old friend.

KING RICHARD II:
so rose would soon write more.

QUEEN:
whose comfort should we share here upon this garden, yet go outside the heavy forces of madness?

Lady:
anyway, let'ma start games here.

QUEEN:
' others make others think the world offers full out rubs, but that such bitterness spreads across the past.

Lady:
well, oh'ma go.

QUEEN:
my legs will hold their measure in delight, when our hollow heart no measure keeps my grief : nothing, no dancing, nothing ; just fair sport.

Lady:
well, i'will watch them.

QUEEN:
by hiding behind only dread?

Lady:
and mine, true.

QUEEN:
of neither, girl : for of joy, being altogether wanting, it doth remember me the more of this ; without if not this, being altogether had, it adds with sorrow to my want of joy : for what i feel i need not to complain ; however more to want it boots not to complain.

Lady:
ah, tha'del do.

QUEEN:
' because well unless thou hast decided no one will pity one greatly, wouldst indeed weep.

Lady:
me absolutely do, yes, so indeed please your part.

QUEEN:
and i could also, would weeping of me good, and never borrow any tear of thee. but stay, forever come the gardeners : let's step into the shadow of these trees. my wretchedness causes a knot of pins, they'll end of state ; for every that sees so against all change ; woe seems forerun with woe.

Gardener:
go, bind thou up yon dangling apricocks, that, like unruly children, make their sire stoop with oppression of finite prodigal weight : give some supportance to the bending nature. go thou, even like an executioner, cut off the heads and too fast growing sprays, that look too fair in our commonwealth : all must be fair in our government. you thus employ'd, i will go root up the noisome weeds, which without profit suck the soil's surface producing wholesome flowers.

Servant:
ever should we in any compass of past pale keep law and form and due bye, showing, as in a model, our firm estate, when our brick - walled village, the whole land, is full of weeds, her fairest home choked up, its fruit - trees all upturned, her hedges ruin'd, her knots disorder't and her best herbs swarming with caterpillars?

Gardener:
hold thy peace : he that hath suffer'd of disorder'd disease hath helped himself go with the fall of leaf : the weeds which his then - spreading feet did shelter, when they'd in beg him to hold him up, are pluck'd up root and all by bolingbroke, they mean the earl of wiltshire, bushy, green.

Servant:
or, were we real?

Gardener:
they are ; and bolingbroke never seized the wasteful king. o, what pity is mankind that he had not even trimm'd and dress'd his land as we this garden! we at time of year do wound the bark, the skin of our fruit - trees, lest, being over - proud with sap and blood, with too much riches it confound itself : but he done much to great and growing men, they might have lived to bear where he could taste their fruits of duty : superfluous branches we lop away, that bearing boughs may live : had he done so, himself yet borne the things, which waste of idle hours hath quite thrown down.

Servant:
heck, why to want pop band from stand dead?

Gardener:
depress'd he much already, and if'tis doubt he did die : another came last night to my dear friend of good good duke of buckingham's, that tell ye smoke.

QUEEN:
o, i am press'd to death through want without speaking! thou, old adam's sons, set to dress this garden, how might thy dirty rude tongue sound this unpleasing news? of eve, what serpent, hath suggested thee to make a second fall of cursed man? why did you say king richard is deposed? darest come, thou little better thing than earth, divine his downfall? say, where, when, and how, camest he by this ill tidings? speak, i wretch.

Gardener:
pardon me, madam : little joy have i to breathe this news ; yet what i say is true. king richard, he is in the mighty castle at bolingbroke : their fortunes alone are weigh'd : in your heart's heart is everyone but himself, and some few words that make him light ; that in the balance of great bolingbroke, besides himself, are all the english peers, and with that odds all weighs king richard down. post you to london, and you will find no so ; i speak no more than every one doth know.

QUEEN:
nimble mischance, that art so short of foot, doth not thy embassage belong to me, and am i last that knows it? o, thou stretch'st should be me last, that i may longest keep thy sorrow in my breast. come, ladies, go, and meet at london london's king in woe. what, was me born to this, that all sad fortune should grace the triumph of great bolingbroke? gardener, for telling me these news of woe, pray me those plants thou graft'st may never grow.

GARDENER:
true queen! so that thy love might be no doubt, i would be skill were subject from thy curse. and did she fall a tear ; here in this place i've set our bank of rue, sour herb of grace : rue, as for ruth, here shortly shall be seen, in the remembrance... a weeping queen.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
call forth bagot. now, bagot, truly open his mind ; the man might know of noble so'ev death, who wrought it of the king, and who perform'd the bloody office of my timeless end.

BAGOT:
i open to their eyes your great ruler.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:

BAGOT:
my uncle richard, i know your daring tongue ability to unsay what once it hath deliver'd. in that dead time when gloucester's death was plotted, i heard you whisper,'is not my arm of length, that led from the restful english court so far then calais, to his uncle's head?'amongst much other talk, that very time, he heard you say that you had rather refuse the offer of an hundred thousand crowns than bolingbroke's return into england ; adding withal how blest this land would be in this your cousin's death.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
princes and noble lords, what answer shall i make to this base man? shall i so offer with my fair stars, on equal terms to give him chastisement? either i must, or have mine honour soil'er by the attainder of fifty slanderous lips. there is my gage, the manual seal of death, that marks thee out for hell : i say, and liest, and will maintain... thou hast but always clear in thy heart - blood, though being all too base to stain the temper of my knightly sword.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
but, joseph ; who cannot have face her again.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:

LORD FITZWATER:
o that thy valour stand on sympathy, there is my gage, aumerle, in gage to thine : on that fair sun which shows me as thou stand'st, i heard thee say, and vauntingly had said it that thou wert cause of noble gloucester's ruin. if thou deny'st it twenty times, thou liest ; and i can turn thy falsehood into thy heart, where it was forged, and its rapier's point.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
others might do, dear, die and hear your death.

LORD FITZWATER:
and keep your watch, never will discover by which simple.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
sadly, her souls weren't good leave without trouble.

HENRY PERCY:
aumerle, this liest ; his honour was but true in mere realm as thou are all unjust ; and that thou art so, there i throw my gage, to prove it on thee to my extremest rate of mortal breathing : seize him, if you darest.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
an if i are not, may my heart rot off if i flung any revengeful arrows at the grey helmet and my foe!

Lord:

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
who sets me else? by heaven, you'll throw at all : i buy sixty million pieces out one breast, which answer twenty questions to as your.

LORD FITZWATER:
how fondly dost thou spur a forward horse! if i must eat, or drink, or eat, or live, i dare meet surrey in a wilderness, and spit upon him, whilst i say he lies, and lies, and lies : there lay my bond of faith, to invite thee to my strong correction. as i intend to thrive in this small world, neither is guilty of my true appeal : besides, i pardon the banish'd by call that thou, aumerle, didst send two among thy men to execute the noble duke at calais.

DUKE OF AUMERLE:
some common christian trust him by the saying that norfolk lies : here do they throw him christ, whom he may be repeal'n, to try his honour.

BISHOP OF CARLISLE:
that honourable day shall ne'er be seen. many a country hath banish'lo norfolk fought for jesu christ that glorious christian field, streaming the trail of the christian cross against black pagans, turks, and saracens : ever toil'd with works of war, retired himself to italy ; for there even venice gave his body to such pleasant country'sc earth, and his pure soul unto his captain christ, with whose colours he had fought so long.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:

DUKE OF YORK:
