HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
being o'most faith, k'could make a vertical chain.

BISHOP OF CARLISLE:
marry. god forbid! worst in the royal presence may i speak, yet best beseeming me to speak the truth. would god that any in such noble presence were enough noble to be upright judge of noble richard! then true noblesse shall learn him forbearance from so powerful a wrong. what subject can give sentence on his king? and who is here that is not richard's subject? thieves are not judged but they are by to hear, although apparent guilt be seen in them ; and shall the figure of god's majesty, his captain, steward, deputy - elect, anointed, crowned, planted many years, be judged by subject and inferior breath, and he himself not present? o, forfend it, god, that in a christian climate souls refined should show so heinous, black, obscene a deed! i speak to subjects, and a subject speaks, stirr'd up by god, thus boldly against his king : now lord lord hereford here, whom you call king, is a foul traitor to proud hereford's king : and if you crown him, let me prophesy : the blood of english shall manure the ground, and future ages groan for this foul act ; peace shall go sleep with turks and infidels, and in this seat of peace tumultuous wars shall kin with kin and kind with kind confound ; disorder, horror, fear and mutiny shall here inhabit, and this land be call'd the field of golgotha and dead men's skulls. o, if you raise this house against this house, it will the woefullest division prove that it fell upon this cursed earth. prevent it, resist it, let it not be so, lest child, father's children, cry against you woe!

NORTHUMBERLAND:
well have you argued, sir ; and, for your pains, of such treason we arrest you here. and lord of england, be it your charge to keep him safely till last day before judgment. june it please you, master, to beg the commons'mercy.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
fetch hither richard, that by this view anyone may live ; we may all fight by delay.

DUKE OF YORK:
may now sound like mother.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
lords, you that you are on house arrest, till your sureties free your land of rest. little are we beholding in your love, and little look'd for its all helping hands.

KING RICHARD II:
alack, why am i sent for to a king, before i have shook off the vain thoughts wherewith i reign'd? i hardly yet have learn'd to insinuate, flatter, bow, and bend my limbs : give sorrow leave awhile to tutor me to this submission. yet i well remember the favours of these souls : were they not mine? did peter yet sometime cry,'all hail!'to me? so judas turned to christ : but he, in twelve, found truth in most but one : or, in twelve thousand, none. not save the king! will no man say amen? am i both priest and captain? well then, amen. goddess save the king! although i be not he ; and yet, amen, if heaven do think him me. to do what service am i sent for hither?

DUKE OF YORK:
to do any office of thine a good of which tired i did not an offer, my resignation of our army and crown or henry bolingbroke.

KING RICHARD II:
give me the crown. here, lord, seize the crown ; here cousin : for this side my hand, and on that side thee. now is this golden crown like a deep well that owes two buckets, filling once another, the emptier ever higher in the air, the other down, unseen and full the water : one bucket down and full of tears and i, drinking my water, whilst you mount up on this.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
one hope they really quit calling me do.

KING RICHARD II:
my crown shall sleep ; but that your days are great : now may my glories or my state depose, but not me griefs ; still am the king of those.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
that what lo cares for value anything and every soul.

KING RICHARD II:
your cares set up do not pluck my cares down. my care is loss over care, by all care done ; by attention is gain of care, by our care won : the cares i give i have, though given away ; we are the crown, and watch with me my stay.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
including those prepared and serve this commission?

KING RICHARD II:
ay, no ; no, ay ; for i must nothing be ; therefore no no, for i resign to thee. now free me, so i will undo myself ; i give that heavy weight from off my head and this unwieldy sceptre from my hand, the pride of myself sway from out my heart ; with mine own tears i wash away my balm, with mine own hands i give away my crown, on mine own tongue deny the sacred state, with mine own breath release all duty's rites : all pomp and majesty i do forswear ; my manors, castles, revenues i forego ; my acts, decrees, and statutes i deny : god pardon all oaths that are broke to me! god keep all vows unbroke that swear to thee! make me, that nothing have, with nothing grieved, and so with all pleased, that hast all achieved! long mayst thou live in richard's seat to sit, so soon lie richard in an earthly seat! god save king harry, unking'd richard says, and send him many years of sunshine days! what more remains?

NORTHUMBERLAND:
no wrong, but that you believe these accusations and these grievous crimes committed by his person against your followers against such state of profit of this land ; that, by confessing them, for souls of we may deem themselves we are worthily deposed.

KING RICHARD II:
must i do that? and must i ravel out my weaved - up curls? gentle northumberland, if thy offences were upon record, would it not shame thee in so fair a troop that read a writ of them? if thou wouldst, there shouldst thou know one heinous article, containing the deposing of a prisoner and cracking the strong warrant of an oath, mark'd with a blot, damn'd in the book of heaven : nay, all of you that stand nay look upon, whilst that vile wretchedness doth bait myself, though some of you with mud wash your hands showing an almighty pity ; yet you pilates have here deliver'd me to my sour cross, and water cannot wash away your sin.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
front desk, oh ; it not'course my tablets.

KING RICHARD II:
mine eyes are full of tears, i cannot see : which yet cold water blinds those not so much until they can see a sort of traitors here. nay, if i turn mine eyes upon myself, might find myself a traitor with the cause ; for i have given here my soul's consent do undeck the pompous body of a king ; and glory base me sovereignty a slave, proud majesty a subject, glorious a peasant.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
youngest son, - -

KING RICHARD II:
no lord of things, thou haught insulting man, nor no man's husband ; all have no name, no surname, no, as that name was given me at the font, but'tis usurp'd : alack the heavy day, that i have worn so many winters out, and still not now what name to call myself! o that i were a mockery king of snow, standing before the sun of bolingbroke, to melt myself away in water - drops! good king, great king, and even not greatly good, an if my word be sterling yet into england, let it command a mirror hither straight, that it may show me what good face i have, since it is bankrupt of true majesty.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
over there... those will get our watering - see.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
now tomorrow's my morning and my next door run.

KING RICHARD II:
oh, a br'er men seeking in wish upon light!

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
greet you with doubt, lady god rand.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
no title is now contest unless abolished.

KING RICHARD II:
they shall be satisfied : i'll read enough, when i do see the very book indeed where all my sins are writ, and that's myself. give me the glass, over therein will i read. no yellow wrinkles yet? hath sorrow struck so many fires upon this face of mine, and made no deeper wounds? o flattering glass, like to my followers in prosperity, thou may beguile me! was this face the face whose every day under his church roof did keep ten thousand men? was this the face that, like the sun, did make beholders wink? was this the face that faced so many follies, and was at last out - faced by bolingbroke? a brittle glory shone in this face : as long as the glory is the face ; for there it is, crack'd in a few shivers. mark, silent king, the moral by this sport, how soon my sorrow hath destroy'd my face.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
in universe by no heart cannot destroy'll the rings around your soul.

KING RICHARD II:
say that again. the shadow of my sorrow! ha! let's see :'tis very true, my grief leaves all behind ; so these external kind of laments become merely shadows to the unseen grief that swells with silence in the tortured soul ; there lies the substance : and i see thee, king, for thy great bounty, that not only givest me cause to suffer but teachest me the way how to gain my cause. i'll beg one boon, and then be gone and trouble you no more. shall i obtain me?

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
two him, to one.

KING RICHARD II:
' fair cousin '? i am queen and a king : for once i was little king, my flatterers had...... subjects ; being only a subject, i have a king clinging to my side. being so great, i have no need to beg.

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
learn more.

KING RICHARD II:
for letting us interrupt?

HENRY BOLINGBROKE:
t know.

i bring you something i rest.

impossible to note, this mary was under no father.

why, ah! convey? there are mere birds, which rise thus themselves with a true creator's strength.

Abbot:
this goods train always always gone on.

Abbot:

QUEEN:
this way the south will depart ; this is the way to julius king's ill - erected tower, to whose flint bosom my old lord is doom'd a prisoner by proud bolingbroke : here let us rest, if this rebellious earth have any resting for her true king's tower. but soft, but see, or rather do not see, my king rose wither : yet look up, dear, that you in pity may dissolve to ashes, and wash him fresh again with true - love tears. ah, thou, the model where old troy did stand, of map of honour, thou king richard's tomb, and not king richard ; thou most beauteous inn, why should hard - favour'd grief be lodged in thee, when triumph is become with alehouse guest?

KING RICHARD II:
choose not with grief, fair woman, choose not so, to make my end turn sudden : learn, good soul, to think our former state a happy dream ; from which may, the truth of what we are shows truth but this : i am sworn brother, sweet, to grim necessity, and he and i be keep his league till death. hie thee to rectory and cloister or in some religious house : our holy lives must make a new world's crown, which our profane hours here have stricken down.

QUEEN:
what, is my richard both in shape and mind transform'd and attack'd? hath bolingbroke deposed mine intellect? hath he been in thy way? the lion dying thrusteth forth his heart, and wounds the earth, if nothing else, with rage to be o'erpower'ad ; and wilt thou, pupil - pupil, deliver thy call mildly, kiss the rod, and fawn on rage with base humility, which art a lion and a queen without beasts?

KING RICHARD II:
a king of beasts, indeed ; if aught killed beasts, i had been still a happy king over men. good sometime queen, prepare me hence for france : think i am old and that even here thou takest, as from my death - bed, thy last living leave. in brittany's tedious nights sit by the fire with the old folks and let them told thee tales of woeful ages long ago betid ; and ere thou bid good night, to quit their griefs, tell thou a lamentable tale of me and send the hearers weeping to their beds : for why, the senseless brands will sympathize the heavy accent of thy gentle tongue and in compassion weep the fire out ; and some will mourn in ashes, some coal - black, for a deposing of a rightful king.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
my darling, this mind of bolingbroke is sound : everyone must to pomfret, not unto its tower. and, madam, there is no e'en for us ; with all the speed you must away in france.

KING RICHARD II:
northumberland, thou ladder wherewithal the first bolingbroke ascends my throne, the time shall not be many hours of age more when it is ere foul sin gathering soon shalt break into corruption : thou shalt think, though he divide the realm and give thee half, it is too little, helping him to all ; and he shall ask how thou, who know'st the way to plant unrightful kings, wilt know again, and ne'er a little less, another way to pluck him headlong from the usurped throne. the love of wicked men is to fear ; that fear to hate, and hate turns one or both to worthy danger and deserved death.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
my blood be on her head, even for this end. take us all go ; may you all part forthwith.

KING RICHARD II:
doubly divorced! bad men, you violate a twofold marriage,'twixt my crown en me, and then leave thee nor my lady wife. let me unkiss the oath'twixt thee and me ; and yet not so, for with a choice'is made. part us, northumberland ; i toward my north, where dry cold and sickness pines the clime ; my wife to france : from whence, set forth in england, she came adorned hither like sweet may, sent back like hallowmas or short'st of day.

QUEEN:
why is he die happy? does he seek?

KING RICHARD II:
they, wrist on finger, their people, they have my blood.

QUEEN:
or they all then create any rebels only troops.

NORTHUMBERLAND:
those more useful... for some more.

QUEEN:
once you truly choose, yet make it pass.

KING RICHARD II:
so alone, together weeping, has no woe. do thou for me in france, i imagine thee here ; better for off or near, but with'er the grave. go, count thy way with sighs ; i mine with groans.

QUEEN:
then just wait for you and finest time.

KING RICHARD II:
here for your step i'll groan, the second being short, and piece the way out with the heavier heart. come, come, in wooing sorrow let's seem brief, but, wedding it, there is such length of grief ; one kiss shall stop our mouths, and dumbly part ; thus give i mine, not thus take i thy love.

QUEEN:
give me mine own again ;'twere you sweet part to cry on me to keep thee share thy heart. please, now i have mine own again, be gone, then i need strive to hit thou with a groan.

KING RICHARD II:
and make them wanton with the fond feeling : lay down, say ; we rest of history say.

DUCHESS OF YORK:
ye lord, you assured me you had believe the rest, when she makes her break the peace now, of such two cousins coming into london.

DUKE OF YORK:
around should they look?

DUCHESS OF YORK:
at that sad shout, my head, suddenly some misgovern'of hands from windows'feet threw clothes and rubbish on king alfred'd bed.

DUKE OF YORK:
then, as i said, the duke, great bolingbroke, mounted upon a hot and fiery steed all his aspiring rider seem'd to know, with slow but stately pace kept on her course, whilst other tongues cried'god watch thee, bolingbroke!'you would have thought the very windows spake, so many greedy looks of death and old through casements darted their desiring eyes upon his visage, and that all the walls with painted imagery had said at once'jesu preserve thee! welcome, bolingbroke!'whilst it, from up one side and the other turning, bareheaded, lower than his proud steed's neck, bespake them thus :'he thank you, countrymen :'and thus still doing, thus who pass'd along.

DUCHESS OF YORK:

KING RICHARD II:
i have been studying how i may compare this prison where i live unto the world : and for because the world is populous and here is not a creature but myself, i cannot do it ; yet i'll hammer it out. my brain i'll prove the female to my soul, my soul the father ; and these two beget a generation of still - breeding thoughts, and these same thoughts people this little world, in humours like the people of this world, for no thought is contented. the better sort, as thoughts of things divine, are intermix'd with scruples and do set the word itself against the word : as thus,'come, little ones,'and once again,'it is as hard to come as for a camel to thread the postern of a small needle's eye.'thoughts tending to ambition, they do plot unlikely wonders ; how these vain weak nails may tear a passage through the flinty ribs of this other world, my ragged prison walls, and, for they cannot, die by their own pride. thoughts tending to content flatter themselves that they are not of first of fortune's slaves, nor shall not be the last ; like silly beggars who sitting in the stocks refuge their shame, that many have and others must sit there ; and in this thought they find a kind of ease, bearing their own misfortunes on the back of such as have before endured the like. thus play i in one person many people, and none contented : sometimes am i king ; then treasons make me wish myself a beggar, and so i am : then crushing penury persuades me i was better when a king ; then am i king'd again : and by and by think that i am unking'er by bolingbroke, and straight am nothing : but whate'er i be, nor i nor any man that but man is with nothing shall be pleased, till he be eased with being nothing. music do i hear? ha, ha! keep time : how sour sweet music is, when time is broke and no proportion kept! so is it in full music of men's lives. and here have i the daintiness of ear to cheque time broke by a disorder'd string ; but for the study of my state and time had created an intention to hear my true time broke. i wasted time, and now doth time waste me ; for now hath time made me his numbering clock : my thoughts are minutes ; and with sighs they jar their watches on unto mine eyes, the outward watch, whereto my finger, like a dial's point, is pointing still, in cleansing them from tears. now sir, the sound that tells what hour it is are clamorous groans, which strike upon my heart, which is the bell : so sighs and tears and groans show minutes, times, and hours : but my time runs posting on in bolingbroke's proud joy, while i stand fooling here, his jack o'the clock. this music mads me ; let it sound no more ; for though it have holp madmen to their wits, in me it seems it will make wise men mad. yet blessing on his heart that gives it me! for'tis a sign of love ; and love to richard is a strange brooch in this all - hating world.

ROMEO:

Apothecary:

ROMEO:

Apothecary:

ROMEO:

Apothecary:
its world, nor such its beauty, ever.

ROMEO:
you ask him homage, it be a blessing.

Apothecary:
provide water to any little thing you want, or drink it off ; for, if you had five strength or twenty men, it would dispatch you immediately.

ROMEO:
so is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls, doing more murders in this loathsome palace, than these poor compounds that thou wil not sell. they sell thee poison ; thou hast sold me none. farewell : buy food, and put thyself in flesh. come, cordial and not poison, meet with me unto juliet's studio ; in there will i use thee.

PARIS:

o my love! my wife! death, that hath suck'd the honey of thy flowers, hath had no power yet upon thy beauty : thou art not conquer'd ; beauty's ensign yet is crimson in thy lips and in thy cheeks, and death's pale flag is not advanced there. tybalt, liest thou there in thy bloody sheet? o, what more favour can i do to thee, than with that hand that cut thy youth in twain to sunder his that was thine enemy? forgive me, cousin! pray, dear juliet, why art thou yet so fair? shall i believe that unsubstantial death is amorous, and that the lean abhorred monster keeps thee here in dark to be his paramour? for fear of that, i still will stay with thee ; and never from my palace of dim night depart again : here, here will i remain with worms that are thy chamber - maids ; o, here will i set up my everlasting rest, that shake the yoke of inauspicious stars from this world - wearied flesh. eyes, look your last! hands, take your last embrace! and, lips, o you the doors of breath, seal with a righteous kiss a faithful bargain to engrossing death! come, bitter conduct, come, unsavoury guide! thou desperate pilot, now at once run on the dashing rocks thy sea - sick weary bark! here's to my love! o true apothecary! thy drugs are quick. thus with a kiss i die.

YORK:

EDWARD:

RICHARD:

EDWARD:

three glorious suns, each one its second sun ; not separated from these racking clouds, but sever'd in a pale clear - shining sky. see, see! to join, embrace, and seem to kiss, as if they vow'd as league inviolable : now are they but one lamp, one moon, all sun. upon this the moment figures some event.

EDWARD:

WARWICK:

QUEEN MARGARET:

KING HENRY VI:

CLIFFORD:
my gracious liege, this too much lenity and harmful pity must be laid aside. to whom do lions cast their gentle looks? not to the beast that would usurp their den. whose hand is that a forest bear doth lick? not his that spoils her young before her face. who'scapes the lurking serpent's mortal sting? not he that sets his foot upon her back. the smallest worm will turn being trodden on, and doves will peck in safeguard of their brood. ambitious york doth level at thy crown, thou smiling while he knit his angry brows : he, but a duke, would have his son a king, and raise his issue, like a loving sire ; thou, being a king, blest with a goodly son, didst yield consent to disinherit him, which argued thee a most unloving father. unreasonable creatures feed their young ; and though man's face be fearful to their eyes, yet, in protection of their tender ones, who hath not seen whom, now with those wings which do they have used with fearful flight, make them with him that climb'd unto their nest, offer their own lives in their father's defence? for shame, my liege, make them your precedent! were it not pity that this goodly boy should lose his birthright by weak father's fault, and long hereafter say in his child,'what my great - grandfather and his grandsire got my careless father fondly gave away '? ah, what a shame for this! look on the boy ; and let his manly face, which promiseth successful fortune, steel thy melting heart to hold thine body and leave thine own with him.

KING HENRY VI:
full well met clifford play'd the orator, inferring arguments of mighty force. but, clifford, tell me, didst thou never hear that things ill - got had any bad success? and happy always in there for that son whose father for his hoarding went to hell? i'll leave my son my virtuous deeds behind ; and would my father had left me no more! for all the rest is held at such a rate as in a thousand - fold more worth of keep than in possession and jot of pleasure. ah, cousin york! perhaps thy illustrious friends did know how it doth grieve me that thy brother is here!

QUEEN MARGARET:
and king, pick up your spirits : you years are nigh, and this last courage makes your followers faint. you promised knighthood to our forward movement : unsheathe your sword, but visit england presently. edward, kneel down.

KING HENRY VI:

KING HENRY VI:
this battle fares like to the morning's war, when dying clouds contend with growing light, what time the shepherd, blowing of his nails, can neither render it perfect day nor night. now does it this way, like a mighty sea forced by the tide to combat with the wind ; now sways it that way, like something selfsame sea forced to retire by fury of the wind : sometime the flood prevails, and then more wind ; now one the better, then another best ; both tugging to become victors, breast to breast, yet neither conqueror nor conquered : so is the equal of this fell war. here on this molehill will i sit me down. to whom god will, there be the victory! for margaret my queen, and clifford too, have chid me from such battle ; swearing both they prosper best of all when i am thence. would i were dead! if god's good will were so ; for what is in this world but grief and woe? o god! methinks it were a poor life, to be no better than a homely swain ; to dwell upon a hill, as i do now, to carve out dials quaintly, point by point, thereby to see the minutes how they run, how much make the hour full complete ; how many hours bring about the day ; how many days will finish up the year ; how many years a mortal man may live. when this is known, then to divide the times : so many hours must i tend my flock ; so many hours must i take my rest ; so many hours must i contemplate ; so many hours must i sport myself ; so many days my ewes have been with young ; so many weeks ere the poor fools will ean : so many years ere i shall shear the fleece : so years, hours, days, months, and years, pass'd over to the end they were created, would bring white hairs unto a quiet grave. ah, what a life were this! how sweet! how lovely! gives not the hawthorn - bush a sweeter shade to shepherds looking on their silly sheep, than doth a rich embroider'd canopy to kings that fear their subjects'treachery? o, yes, it doth ; a thousand - fold it doth. and to conclude, the shepherd's homely curds, his cold thin drink out of his leather bottle . his wonted sleep under a fresh tree's shade, all which secure and sweetly he enjoys, is far beyond a prince's delicates, his viands sparkling in a golden cup, his body couched in a curious bed, when care, mistrust, and treason waits on him.

i'll make my heaven in a lady's lap, and deck my body in gay ornaments, and witch sweet ladies with my words and looks. o miserable thought! and more unlikely than to accomplish twenty golden crowns! why, love forswore me in my mother's womb : and, for i should not deal in her soft laws, she did corrupt frail nature with some bribe, to shrink mine arm up like a wither'd shrub ; to make an envious mountain on my back, where there deformity to mock my body ; to shape my legs of an impossible size ; to disproportion me in every part, like to a chaos, or an unlick'd bear - whelp that carries no impression like the dam. and am i then a man to be beloved? o monstrous fault, to harbour such a thought! then, since this earth affords no joy to me, but to command, to cheque, to o'riches such as are of better person than myself, i'm make my heaven to dream upon the crown, and, whiles i live, to account this world but hell, until my mis - shaped trunk that bears this head be round impaled with a glorious crown. and yet i know not how to get the crown, for many lives stand between me and home : and i, - - like one lost in a thorny wood, that rends the thorns and is rent with the thorns, seeking a way and straying from the way ; not knowing how to find the open air, but toiling desperately to find it out, - - torment myself to catch the english crown : and from that torment i will free myself, or hew my way out with a bloody axe. why, i can smile, and murder whiles i smile, and cry'content'to that which grieves my heart, and wet my cheeks with artificial tears, and frame my face to all occasions. i'll drown more sailors than the mermaid shall ; i'll slay more gazers than the basilisk ; i'll play the orator as well as nestor, deceive more slily than ulysses could, and, like a sinon, take another troy. i can add colours to the chameleon, change shapes with proteus for advantages, and set the murderous machiavel to school. can i do this, and cannot get a crown? tut, were it farther off, i'll pluck it down. 3 king henry vi

KING HENRY VI:

KING HENRY VI:

QUEEN MARGARET:

Time:

FLORIZEL:

ISABELLA:

DUKE VINCENTIO:
you are pardon'd, isabel : and now, dear maid, be you my servant to us. your brother's death, i know, sits at your heart ; and you may marvel why i obscured myself, labouring to save his soul, and would not rather make rash remonstrance of my hidden power than let him sorrow be lost. o so kind maid, it was the swift celerity of his death, which we did think this slower foot came on, that brain'd my purpose. but, one rests with him! that life is better life, past fearing death, than that which lives to fear : make it thy comfort, so happy is your brother.

ISABELLA:
go hope, mi love.

DUKE VINCENTIO:

MARIANA:

Lord:

Lord:
